                .addSize([320, 100], [[300, 50], [320, 50]])
                .addSize([740, 100], [728, 90])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/anie.202410080')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6cb887953d824c07baa3da05f2e5341f">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'eede6164-39da-4973-bc5e-bc7eab840f1a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                     <p>Haihe Laboratory of Sustainable Chemical Transformations, Tianjin, 300192 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhang/Kai">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Chen/Jun" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a15" data-db-target-for="a15" aria-controls="a15" aria-haspopup="true" id="a15_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Prof. Jun Chen</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a15" aria-labelledby="a15_Ctrl" role="region" id="a15">
                     <p class="author-name">Prof. Jun Chen</p>
                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Advanced Chemical Power Sources, Key Laboratory of Advanced Energy Materials Chemistry (Ministry of Education), Collaborative Innovation Center of Chemical Science and Engineering (Tianjin), Renewable Energy Conversion and Storage Center (RECAST), College of Chemistry, Nankai University, Tianjin, 300071 China</p>
                     <p>Haihe Laboratory of Sustainable Chemical Transformations, Tianjin, 300192 China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Chen/Jun">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d49508360" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A surface gradient desodiation strategy is proposed to solve capacity decay and voltage drop without compromising the initial specific capacity of layered cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. This tactic not only inhibits transition-metal-ion migration at high voltage, but also endows a stable interface by reducing the generation of Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Co<sup>4+</sup> ions on the surface of the cathode materials.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a16687bf-3766-440c-8975-fd81d686f85e/anie202410080-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a16687bf-3766-440c-8975-fd81d686f85e/anie202410080-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b8711b75-8cf3-48bd-ae4a-38821f1bf49f/anie202410080-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a16687bf-3766-440c-8975-fd81d686f85e/anie202410080-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d49508374" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a promising technology for large-scale energy storage have received unprecedented attention. However, the cathodes in SIBs generally suffer from detrimental cathode-electrolyte interfacial side reactions and structural degradation during cycling, which leads to severe capacity fade and voltage decay. Here, we have developed an ultra-stable Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.20</sub>Co<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.036</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM-CS-GMg) cathode material in which a Mg-free core is encapsulated by a shell with gradient distribution of Mg using coprecipitation method with Mg-hysteretic cascade feedstock followed by calcination. From the interior to outer surface of the shell, as the content of electrochemically inactive Mg gradually increases, the Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation amount gradually decreases after charged. Benefiting from this surface gradient desodiation, the surface transition metal (TM) ion migration from TM layers to Na layers is effectively inhibited, thus suppressing the layered-to-rock-salt phase transition and the resultant microcracks. Besides, the less formation of high-valence TM ions on the surface contributes to a stable cathode-electrolyte interface. The as-prepared NCM-CS-GMg exhibits remarkable cycling life over 3000 cycles with a negligible voltage drop (0.127 mV per cycle). Our findings highlight an effective way to developing sustainable cathode materials without compromising on the initial specific capacity for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410080-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410080-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant interests as a promising technology for grid-scale energy storage systems due to the abundant Na resources and the similar electrochemical mechanism to lithium-ion batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0001_R_d49508345e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0003_R_d49508345e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a></sup></span> As cathodes play an important role in determining the battery performance, numerous efforts have been dedicated to exploiting the cathode materials with long cycle life and high energy density for SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0004_R_d49508345e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">4</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0006_R_d49508345e597" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span> Among various candidates, sodium manganese-based transition metal oxides (Na<sub>x</sub>TM<sub>1−y</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 0<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2e31b503-6a64-4266-8dd9-206c5f9ff497/anie202410080-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
x≤1, y&gt;0.5, TM=Ni, Co, Cu, Fe et al.), have been one of the most attractive cathodes because of their open Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels, high theoretical capacities and cost advantage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0007_R_d49508345e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0008_R_d49508345e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Nevertheless, Na<sub>x</sub>TM<sub>1-y</sub>Mn<sub>y</sub>O<sub>2</sub> generally suffer from complicated surface/interfacial chemistry and unexpected phase evolution during charging.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0009_R_d49508345e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">9</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0011_R_d49508345e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">11</a></sup></span> Especially, at highly charged state, the high oxidation state TM ions (e.g., Ni<sup>4+</sup>) exacerbate the side reaction, such as TM-ion dissolution and gas generation (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0001">1a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0012_R_d49508345e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Joule">12</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0015_R_d49508345e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a></sup></span> Moreover, TM-ion migration from the TM layers to the Na layers occurs at the cathode/electrolyte interface, leading to a phase transition from layered phase to rock-salt phase (surface reconstruction).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0016_R_d49508345e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Acc. Chem. Res.">16</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0017_R_d49508345e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> Then, the gradual build-up strain at the newly formed heterointerface subsequently induces fatal intragranular microcracks.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0018_R_d49508345e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0019_R_d49508345e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">19</a></sup></span> The above-mentioned issues finally result in rapid capacity decay and severe voltage drop during long-term cycles.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0020_R_d49508345e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Nat. Energy">20</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0022_R_d49508345e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Commun.">22</a></sup></span> Therefore, it is essential to control the valence state of surface TM ions and prevent the TM-ion migration, which can be realized through limiting the Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation amount on the surface.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410080-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c1a8cca3-35ff-4a16-8bf4-45976c7820f8/anie202410080-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c1a8cca3-35ff-4a16-8bf4-45976c7820f8/anie202410080-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/58a91084-3505-4b91-942e-450a7658a9d1/anie202410080-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c1a8cca3-35ff-4a16-8bf4-45976c7820f8/anie202410080-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410080-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>(a) Schematic illustration of the degradation mechanism in NCM. (b) Surface gradient desodiation structural design concept for NCM-CS-GMg.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Introducing electrochemically inactive cations (such as Mg<sup>2+</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0023_R_d49508345e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span> Al<sup>3+</sup>,<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0024_R_d49508345e691" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span> Ti<sup>4+[25]</sup> and Zn<sup>2+[26]</sup>) is an effective method to anchor more Na<sup>+</sup> ions residing in the Na layer of layered oxide cathodes upon charge. Unfortunately, the inactive cations doping not only reduces the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction amount on the surface of cathode materials, but also decreases the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation in its bulk. This inevitably results in significant capacity loss. Thus, rational structural design such as core–shell structure or surface oxides coating is a feasible way to reduce the content of inactive metal.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0027_R_d49508345e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Mater. Today Energy">27</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0029_R_d49508345e705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">29</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, structural mismatch between core and shell typically occurs in core–shell architectures and metal oxide coated cathodes, resulting in the gradual accumulated lattice strain at the interface and even core–shell separation after long-term cycling. It is now urgent to find a way to regulate the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction at the surface without introducing extra interface, ensuring the durability of the protective shell.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we develop a Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.20</sub>Co<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.036</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (demoted as NCM-CS-GMg) cathode material with Mg-free core and Mg doped shell where Mg content decreases from outer surface to interior. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations, focused ion beam (FIB) combined with energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) techniques demonstrate that NCM-CS-GMg exhibits surface gradient desodiation character after charged. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0001">1b</a>, the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from the Na layers gradually decreases as the Mg content increases from the interior to the outer surface of the shell. Adequate Na<sup>+</sup> ions at the surface region serve as pillars to resist TM-ion migration from TM layers to Na layers and reduce the formation of high valence TM ions. Aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy (AC-STEM) reveals that the TM-ion migration, heterogeneous surface reconstruction and resultant microcracks are successfully suppressed. The electron energy loss spectroscopy (EELS) spectra combined with the multiple linear least-squares (MLLS) quantitative analysis illustrate that the activation of low-voltage Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>2+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup>/Mn<sup>3+</sup> couples and the TM-ion dissolution are significantly mitigated. This work suggests a gradient desodiation behavior for constructing layered oxide cathode materials with ultra-long cycle life for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410080-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410080-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Material structure analysis</b>. Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM), Na<sub>0.71</sub>Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.044</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM-Mg), Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.041</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM-CS-Mg) and Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.20</sub>Co<sub>0.21</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.036</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NCM-CS-GMg) were prepared by a facile and scalable coprecipitation method using different feeding methods (see detail in the Experimental Section). The chemical compositions are confirmed by inductively coupled plasma emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) (Table S1). The component on the surface of NCM-CS-GMg precursor was probed by the Electron Probe X-ray Micro-Analyzer (EPMA) (Figure S1, Table S2). The measured atomic ratio of Mn : Ni : Co : Mg is 0.55 : 0.18 : 0.23 : 0.043. To validate the elemental distribution of NCM-CS-GMg precursor, representative spheres were cleaved to semi-spheres by FIB technology, as shown in Figure S2. The EDS line scan data (inset of Figure S2) shows that the core possesses uniformly dispersed Mn, Ni, and Co, and is free of Mg. In the shell, from interior to outer surface, the contents of Mn Ni, and Co gradually decrease, while the content of Mg gradually increases. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2a</a> and Figure S3 show the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the as-synthesized cathodes. All diffraction peaks of these samples can be well indexed to the hexagonal P2 phase with space group <i>P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc</i> (crystal structure as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2a</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0030_R_d49508345e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - eScience">30</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0031_R_d49508345e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - SmartMat.">31</a></sup></span> Rietveld refinement has been carried out to analyze the crystal parameters and atomic position (Table S3). The doped Mg ions lie in TM layer and the unit cell of NCM minor contracts along c-axis after Mg substitution. All of the prepared materials present the spherical-like morphology, as shown in scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2b</a>, Figure S4). Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2c</a> and Figure S5 exhibit the FIB-EDS results of cleaved NCM-CS-GMg and NCM-CS-Mg semi-spheres. The NCM-CS-GMg is composed of a Mg-free core and Mg-gradient shell (inset of Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2c</a>), which is consistent with its precursor. As a comparison, the Mg concentration in the shell of NCM-CS-Mg sample (inset of Figure S5) exhibits sudden elevation at the core–shell interface and then retains constant in the shell, which corresponds to our design.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410080-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/11c57da4-1969-435f-ac62-bc62e04ddee8/anie202410080-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/11c57da4-1969-435f-ac62-bc62e04ddee8/anie202410080-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/cc8d6538-555e-4503-b4fc-34588eab96fd/anie202410080-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/11c57da4-1969-435f-ac62-bc62e04ddee8/anie202410080-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410080-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Crystal structure, micromorphology and electronic structure of NCM-CS-GMg: (a) XRD pattern and corresponding Rietveld refinement (The insert map is the crystal structure model of P2 phase). (b) SEM image. (c) Elementary-line scanning of the cross-section from the shell to the core regions. (d) STEM-HAADF image of a thin slice including the shell and the core of the secondary spherical particle prepared by the FIB technology (The dark red dashed lines are used to distinguish the shell and the core of the secondary spherical particle. A Pt protective layer was deposited during the FIB thinning process). (e) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF lattice image of the particle in the core region (the capture area is marked by the blue rectangle in(d)). (f) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF lattice image of the grain in the shell region (the capture area is marked by the orange rectangle in(d)). (g–i) The representative EELS spectra of (g) Mn L-edge, (h) Co L-edge and (i) Ni L-edge. The line scan direction and acquisition position are marked by the orange arrow and numbers in (d).                </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further confirm the Mg distribution, a thin slice of NCM-CS-GMg prepared by FIB technology (Figure S6) is characterized by AC-STEM (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2d</a>–<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">f</a>). The high angle annular dark field (HAADF) images of core structure and shell structure regarding (002) crystal plane are showed in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2e</a> and Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2f</a>, respectively (corresponding capture regions are marked in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2d</a>). The heavy TM atoms appear as bright-dot contrasts, whereas the Na and O atoms are indiscernible due to their low atomic weights. There are no ions detected in Na layers in HAADF image of the shell region of NCM-CS-GMg, which is consistent with the result of XRD Rietveld refinement showing that the Mg ions reside in TM layer. The interlayer lattice distance between the adjacent TM layers is measured to be 5.55 Å and 5.52 Å for the core and the shell of NCM-CS-GMg, respectively. Besides, NCM exhibits similar interlayer lattice distance of 5.54 Å (Figure S7).</p>
         
         <p>The EELS analysis was performed to determine the chemical valence states of TMs in NCM-CS-GMg. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2g</a>–<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">i</a> shows the representative EELS spectra of Mn L-edge, Co L-edge and Ni L-edge, respectively. States 1–6 in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2g</a>–<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">i</a> correspond to the arrow marks in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0002">2d</a>. The peaks of Mn L-edge and Co L-edge spectra present no significant shift, while the peaks of Ni L-edge shift to the lower energy from the shell to the core region, indicating the decreasing Ni valence state. Corresponding MLLS quantitative analysis (Figure S8a–f) demonstrates that Mn and Co always keep at +4 and +2.7, respectively. Nevertheless, the valence state of Ni changes from +2.1 to +2 from the shell to the core regions (Figure S8g–i), because the substitution of Co with low-valence Mg increases the Ni valence for the charge neutrality. In comparison, the valence states of Mn, Co and Ni for the NCM from the surface to the center retain at +4, +2.7 and +2, respectively (Figure S9, S10).</p>
         
         <p><b>Electrochemical performance</b>. The electrochemical performances of NCM, NCM-Mg, NCM-CS-Mg and NCM-CS-GMg were evaluated in half-cell within the voltage window of 2–4.3 V. The initial galvanostatic charge and discharge (GCD) profiles at 0.2 C are depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3a</a>. The initial charge/discharge capacities and the corresponding charge transfer number per formula are measured as follows: NCM (118/109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.43), NCM-Mg (94/94 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.36), NCM-CS-Mg (101/103 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.40) and NCM-CS-GMg (112/107 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 0.42). Obviously, the NCM-CS-GMg delivers the highest initial discharge specific capacity among the Mg-doped cathodes. Besides, the initial discharge capacities of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg within the voltage range of 1.5–4.3 V are 167.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 164.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S11), respectively, indicating that capacity loss of NCM-CS-GMg is almost negligible. The dQ/dV curves corresponding to the initial GCD profiles are shown in Figure S12a–d. The peaks located at below 4 V are ascribed to the Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> and Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox couples, and those at ~4.2 V are attributed to the Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup> redox couple.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0032_R_d49508345e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0033_R_d49508345e921" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">33</a></sup></span> The peaks located at 3–4 V exhibit higher redox potential in NCM-CS-GMg compared with NCM. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3b</a> presents the cycling performances of NCM, NCM-Mg, NCM-CS-Mg, and NCM-CS-GMg at 0.2 C, and the capacity retentions after 150 cycles are 69 %, 83 %, 78 % and 89 %, respectively. The Mg-gradient-shell contributes to the cycling lifespan of P2-type manganese-based layered oxide and high intial coulombic efficiency. However, the sudden change in composition at the core–shell interface in the NCM-CS-Mg usually causes the formation of voids and even the core–shell separation during repeated charge/discharge processes, resulting in capacity fading.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0034_R_d49508345e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0035_R_d49508345e927" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Mater.">35</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410080-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9b06f34e-56d4-4f3f-be14-28a773027120/anie202410080-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9b06f34e-56d4-4f3f-be14-28a773027120/anie202410080-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/65da80d5-251c-4cd8-813c-78cf2109bab0/anie202410080-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9b06f34e-56d4-4f3f-be14-28a773027120/anie202410080-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410080-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Electrochemical performances of prepared cathodes: (a) Initial charge/discharge profiles and (b) cycling performances of NCM, NCM-Mg, NCM-CS-Mg and NCM-CS-GMg at 0.2 C. (c) Normalized voltage profile variation for NCM and NCM-CS-GMg from 1st to 150th cycles. (d) Average discharge voltages fading of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg during the electrochemical cycling. (e) Long-term cycling performance of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg at 10 C after 3000 cycles. (f) Comparison of cycling performance between this work and the previously reported Mn-based layered oxide cathodes for SIBs. (g) Cycling performance of NCM-CS-GMg//hard carbon full cell at 0.5 C.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3c</a>,<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">d</a> exhibits the comparison of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg upon voltage decay behaviors. The voltage fading of the NCM-CS-GMg after 150 cycles is significantly mitigated (only 41 mV), which is less than that of NCM (140 mV). The average discharge voltages of NCM-CS-GMg are higher than those of NCM during 150 cycles. This is because the d-band centers of Ni and Co in NCM-CS-GMg are located further away from the Fermi level compared to those in NCM, resulting in a higher redox potential for Ni and Co in NCM-CS-GMg (Figure S12). The long-term cycling properties of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg tested at 10 C are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3e</a> and Figure S13. Both NCM and NCM-CS-GMg could deliver a discharge capacity of 80 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C. During the initial 500 cycles, NCM-CS-GMg exhibits better cycling stability compared to NCM. Subsequently, the discharge capacity of NCM shows rapid fading, while the NCM-CS-GMg maintains stable cycling performance with a minimal capacity decay rate (0.01 % per cycle) and voltage drop (0.127 mV per cycle) in 3000 cycles. As indicated in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3f</a>, NCM-CS-GMg cathode shows an overwhelming advantage in cycling performance compared to the reported P2-type sodium oxide cathodes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0008_R_d49508345e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">8</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0033_R_d49508345e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">33</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0036_R_d49508345e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Commun.">36</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0048_R_d49508345e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></sup></span> Besides, the NCM-CS-GMg also exhibits outstanding rate performance (Figure S14). Even at a high current density of 25 C, the reversible discharge capacity can reach 65 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, standing out as one of the top performers among Mn-based layered oxides (Figure S15).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0032_R_d49508345e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0033_R_d49508345e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">33</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0044_R_d49508345e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0049_R_d49508345e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Mater.">49</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0052_R_d49508345e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span> After the current density is set back to 0.2 C, the capacity can recover to 110 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the application feasibility of NCM-CS-GMg cathode, a full cell was assembled with NCM-CS-GMg cathode and commercial HC anode, denoted as NCM-CS-GMg//HC (Figure S16a). The electrochemical performances of HC anode in the voltage range of 0.01–2 V are shown in Figure S16b. The charge–discharge profiles of NCM-CS-GMg cathode and HC anode are displayed in Figure S16c. As shown in Figure S16d and Figure S17, the full cell exhibits a specific capacity of 99.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C and decent rate performance with a specific capacity of 77.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 C (based on the cathode active mass). The corresponding Ragone plot (energy density vs. power density) is presented in Figure S18a. A high energy density of 290.7 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at a power density of 58.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup> can be achieved. Besides, it can also exhibit a high power density of 2788.9 W kg<sup>−1</sup> with an outstanding energy density of 241.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. The high power density can be attributed to the exceptional rate performance and high average operating voltage (Figure S18b). A high output voltage of 3.22 V at a power density of 58.1 W kg<sup>−1</sup> can be realized with a slow decay rate as the power density increases to 2788.9 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Subsequently, the long-term cycling stability test of the full cell was carried out at 0.5 C within the voltage range of 1.9–4.2 V. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0003">3g</a> and Figure S19, the NCM-CS-GMg//HC full cell exhibits fabulous cycling stability with a capacity retention of 75 % after 300 cycles, which outperforms those of the previously reported layered cathode and HC full cell systems.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0032_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">32</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0039_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">39</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0044_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0048_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0053_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Commun.">53</a>-<a href="#anie202410080-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0056_R_d49508345e1003" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Energy Storage Mater.">56</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p><b>Surface gradient desodiation character</b>. In order to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> distribution of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg at the first charged state, the FIB coupled with STEM-HAADF/EDS techniques was employed. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">d</a> shows the STEM-HAADF images and the EDS line scan results. The residual Na<sup>+</sup> ions are evenly distributed in the NCM (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4a</a>,<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">b</a>), while they gradually decrease from the surface to the core regions in the NCM-CS-GMg (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4c</a>,<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">d</a>). In terms of the quantitative analysis by EDS, the ratio of Na to TM ions for NCM is 0.26 (Figure S20). The ratios of Na to TM ion for the particles in the shell region and the core region for NCM-CS-GMg are 0.36 and 0.28, respectively (Figure S21). The lattice spacing of (002) facet gradually expands with the extraction of electrostatic shielding Na<sup>+</sup> from the Na layers upon charging. Three positions were selected from the NCM-CS-GMg slice, specifically from the surface, the middle of the shell, and the core region, respectively, to obtain the atomic arrangement along the [010] axis (Figure S22). The measured values of lattice spacing of (002) planes increase from 5.66 Å (the surface region) to 5.71 Å (the core region) (Figure S22, Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4e</a>), demonstrating the gradient distribution characteristic of Na<sup>+</sup> in the charged NCM-CS-GMg.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410080-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ec41bd96-60a1-44c3-8045-4b6d47243d87/anie202410080-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ec41bd96-60a1-44c3-8045-4b6d47243d87/anie202410080-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/82659dbe-8aec-4d2c-99ad-0a82d2a40dce/anie202410080-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ec41bd96-60a1-44c3-8045-4b6d47243d87/anie202410080-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410080-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Desodiation behaviors and corresponding charge compensation for NCM and NCM-CS-GMg cathodes at the first charged state: (a–d) The STEM-HAADF images and corresponding EDS line scan results of (a–b) NCM and (c–d) NCM-CS-GMg. (e) The comparison of lattice spacing for (002) planes in the surface, the middle of the shell, and the core region, respectively. (f) The DFT calculation result of equilibrium voltages for Na<sub>28−n</sub>Ni<sub>8</sub>Co<sub>10</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>O<sub>80</sub>, Na<sub>28−n</sub>Ni<sub>7</sub>Co<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>O<sub>80</sub> and Na<sub>28−n</sub>Ni<sub>6</sub>Co<sub>8</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>Mg<sub>4</sub>O<sub>80</sub> (from the left to the right). The intermediate phases corresponding to the lower and upper limits of average voltages are <i>n</i>=17 and <i>n</i>=18, respectively. (g–i) XPS characterization of (g) Ni 2p, (h) Co 2p and (i) Mn 2p for the both cathode materials. The scale bars in (a) and (c) are both 2 μm.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>DFT calculation further reveals the surface gradient desodiation character of NCM-CS-GMg. The voltage required for the equimolar extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from the host structure could be calculated upon the charge.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0057_R_d49508345e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0058_R_d49508345e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Phys. Rev. Appl.">58</a></sup></span> The 5×8×1 supercells of Na<sub>0.72−x</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Na<sub>28−n</sub>Ni<sub>8</sub>Co<sub>10</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>O<sub>80</sub>), Na<sub>0.72−x</sub>Ni<sub>0.19</sub>Co<sub>0.24</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Na<sub>28−n</sub>Ni<sub>7</sub>Co<sub>9</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>Mg<sub>2</sub>O<sub>80</sub>), and Na<sub>0.72−x</sub>Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>Mg<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (Na<sub>28−n</sub> Ni<sub>6</sub>Co<sub>8</sub>Mn<sub>22</sub>Mg<sub>4</sub>O<sub>80</sub>) (0≤x≤0.72, 0≤n≤28) are built to explore the desodiation character of NCM-CS-GMg. The <i>x</i>=0.45 (<i>n</i>=17) and <i>x</i>=0.47 (<i>n</i>=18), which correspond to the theoretical specific capacities of 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for Na<sub>0.72</sub>Ni<sub>0.2</sub>Co<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, respectively, are selected as the lower and upper limits of these three compositions for calculating the average voltages. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4e</a>, the obtained values of average voltage increase from 4.05 V to 4.31 V with the raising of Mg content, which demonstrates the gradually increasing energy barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. This calculation result reflects that when the same upper voltage (e.g., 4.3 V) is set, the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> extraction from the host structure decreases as the content of Mg increases.</p>
         
         <p>The ex situ x-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out to explore the effect of surface gradient desodiation on the charge compensation. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4g</a>,<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">h</a>, when charged to 4.3 V, the contents of Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Co<sup>4+</sup> in NCM are 66 % and 78 %, respectively, which are higher than those in NCM-CS-GMg (54 % and 58 %). More residual Na<sup>+</sup> at the surface region of the charged NCM-CS-GMg results in the less formation of Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Co<sup>4+</sup>, which mitigates the surface activity and side reactions between cathode material and electrolyte at high voltage. When discharged to 2 V, the valences of Ni and Co in both materials are reduced to +2 and +3, respectively, indicating their contribution of charge compensation during the initial discharge process (Figure S23a,b). The Mn<sup>4+</sup> and O<sup>2−</sup> in both materials have few capacity contribution during the first cycle (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0004">4i</a>, Figure S23c and Figure S24).</p>
         
         <p><b>Structural and interfacial stability</b>. In situ XRD technique was performed to monitor the long-range structural evolutions of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg during the initial charge and discharge process in the voltage window of 2–4.3 V at 0.2 C. The corresponding lattice parameter variations were investigated by Rietveld refinement (Figure S25). No new peaks and peak splitting are observed for both NCM and NCM-CS-GMg, indicating their stability of the P2 phase structure during (de)sodiation. Furthermore, the volume change of both NCM and NCM-CS-GMg is calculated to be 0.65 %.</p>
         
         <p>The AC-STEM technique was applied to precisely identify the local structural transformation. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5a</a> shows the low-magnification STEM-HAADF images of the NCM after 150 cycles. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a1–a4 exhibits STEM-HAADF images of the internal grain. Intragranular microcracks with lengths of 10–20 nm are intensively distributed along the edge of the internal particle of the NCM (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a1). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a2, the inhomogeneous surface reconstruction (layered to rock-salt phase transitions) is detected on the edge of the microcrack in the atomic resolution STEM-HAADF image viewed along [010] zone axes (the corresponding capture region is marked by the blue rectangle in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a1). The orientation of the microcracks aligns with the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion direction. Besides, TM-ion migration from the TM layers to the Na layers is observed in the transition region between the P2 layered and the rock-salt phases. Enlarged views of the TM-ion migration region and rock-salt region are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a3 and Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a4, respectively. Meanwhile, the STEM-HAADF images of the outer grain (marked by the orange rectangle in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5a</a>) of the NCM are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a5–a8. The microcracks along the edge of the outer grain are longer than those in the internal particles, with an average length of 50 nm. The TM-ion migration and rock-salt phase are also visible in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>a6–a8. It can be seen clearly that the TM-ion migration results in inhomogeneous surface reconstruction. Then build-up of high interfacial lattice strain between layer and rock-salt phases further leads to the formation of microcracks during cycling. In addition, the surface reconstruction initiating from the cathode surface gradually propagates into the bulk structure, which results in more serious microcracks of the outer particles than those in the internal particles. This phenomenon is consistent with the results in previous studies.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0018_R_d49508345e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0059_R_d49508345e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Nat. Commun.">59</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0060_R_d49508345e1285" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Mater.">60</a></sup></span> No obvious microcrack is detected on the primary particles in both the core and the shell regions of the NCM-CS-GMg thin slice (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5b</a>) after 150 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>b1–b3, Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>b5–b7). As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>b3, b4 and Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5</a>b7, b8, the (002) crystal planes of primary particles in the shell and inner core regions both only display slight lattice contractions after repeated (de)sodiation processes, exhibiting excellent structural stability. This is because sufficient residual Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the shell work as pillars to hinder the TM migration at the deeply desodiated state. XRD was employed to detect the long-range structural stability of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg after 150 cycles. The crystal structure of NCM-CS-GMg is well preserved, whereas the crystallinity of NCM is severely decreased, implying that the local structure disorder will destroy the long-range structural stability after long-term cycling (Figure S26).
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202410080-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/209a7f82-d42d-488b-8fe5-b1f6c12f3f64/anie202410080-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/209a7f82-d42d-488b-8fe5-b1f6c12f3f64/anie202410080-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/97f7cd8a-d45d-494c-81a8-f2e8be2afdb9/anie202410080-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/209a7f82-d42d-488b-8fe5-b1f6c12f3f64/anie202410080-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410080-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410080" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Evolutions of structure and valence state for NCM and NCM-CS-GMg cathodes after 150 cycles: (a) STEM-HAADF image of a thin slice including the surface and the interior of the NCM secondary spherical particle prepared by the FIB technology. (a1) Enlarged STEM-HAADF image of the blue rectangle in (a). (a2) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF image of the region marked in the blue rectangle in (a1). (a3) Enlarged image of TM-ion migration region in (a2). (a4) Enlarged image of rock-salt phase in (a2). (a5) Enlarged STEM-HAADF image of the orange rectangle in (a). (a6) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF image of the region marked in the orange rectangle in (a5). (a7) Enlarged image of TM-ion migration region in (a5). (a8) Enlarged image of rock-salt phase in (a5). (b) STEM-HAADF image of a thin slice including the shell and the core of the NCM-CS-GMg secondary spherical particle prepared by the FIB technology. (b1) Enlarged STEM-HAADF image of the blue rectangle in (b). (b2) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF image of the region marked in the blue rectangle in (b1). (b3) Enlarged image of the white dashed box in (b2). (b4) Intensity line profiles obtained from (b2). (b5) Enlarged STEM-HAADF image of the orange rectangle in (b). (b6) Atomic resolution STEM-HAADF image of the region marked in the orange rectangle in (b5). (b7) Enlarged image of the white dashed box in (b6). (b8) Intensity line profiles obtained from (b6). (c–f) Mn L-edge EELS spectra of (c) NCM and (e) NCM-CS-GMg cathodes. Percentage distribution of Mn<sup>2+</sup>, Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> by the MLLS quantitative analysis for (d) NCM and (f) NCM-CS-GMg cathodes. (The corresponding EELS scanning pathway is shown in Figure S29). (g) TM ions concentration detected on the Na anode of both cathodes after 150 cycles. The scale bars in (a) and (b) are both 1 μm. The scale bars in (a1), (a5), (b1), and (b5) are 20 nm. The scale bars in (a2–a4), (a6–a8), (b2–b3), and (b6–b7) are 1 nm.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The operando differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) was employed to monitor the CO<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub> generation for NCM and NCM-CS-GMg during the first cycle. The number of CO<sub>2</sub> released due to electrolyte oxidation and residual carbonate decomposition is considered to be an indicator of the cathode-electrolyte interface side reactions under high voltage.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0013_R_d49508345e1344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - InfoMat.">13</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure S27a, the amount of CO<sub>2</sub> for the NCM-CS-GMg is reduced under high voltage compared to that in the NCM. No O<sub>2</sub> gas generation is detected in both samples, demonstrating the absence of lattice oxygen release (Figure S27b).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0061_R_d49508345e1352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">61</a></sup></span> The near-surface chemical compositions after 150 cycles were investigated by XPS analysis. A weak NaF peak is recorded for NCM-CS-GMg, but none for NCM, which indicates reduced fluoroethylene carbonate additive decomposition (Figure S28a).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0014_R_d49508345e1355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0062_R_d49508345e1355" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">62</a></sup></span> The relatively weaker intensities of the C−O, C=O and OCO<sub>2</sub> peaks for NCM-CS-GMg imply a lower carbonate electrolyte decomposition amount (Figure S28b).</p>
         
         <p>The depth EELS combined with MLLS analysis was performed to quantitatively access the valence evolution of Mn and Co of NCM and NCM-CS-GMg after 150 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5c</a>–<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">f</a> and Figure S29–S34). As the valence of Ni has been reduced to +2 when first discharged to 2 V, suggesting the reversible Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Ni<sup>3+</sup>/Ni<sup>2+</sup> redox. The EELS scanning pathways are marked in Figure S29 by orange arrows. The Mn L-edge EELS spectra of NCM display a negative shift and shape variations from 70 nm deep to the surface (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5c</a>). MLLS analysis results indicate that the reduction of Mn<sup>4+</sup> is detected at 40 nm deep in the NCM, where 33 % of Mn<sup>4+</sup> has been reduced to Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5d</a>, Figure S30). Then the content of Mn<sup>2+</sup> gradually increases and the Mn<sup>4+</sup> is completely reduced to Mn<sup>2+</sup> within 10 nm deep to the surface. This inhomogeneous distribution of the valence state of Mn from the inter to the surface is ascribed to the unstable interface chemistry. The aggravated electrolyte oxidation and HF generation by massive Ni<sup>4+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> at highly desodiated state trigger the formation of Mn<sup>3+</sup> species in NCM. Mn<sup>3+</sup> will further react with acidic species and disproportionation reactions happens to form Mn<sup>2+</sup> during subsequent cycling.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0018_R_d49508345e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nat. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0063_R_d49508345e1409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">63</a></sup></span> In contrast, apart from a few Mn<sup>2+</sup> within 10 nm to the surface, a uniform Mn<sup>4+</sup> distribution is observed throughout the 150-cycled NCM-CS-GMg particle (Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5e</a>,<a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">f</a>, Figure S31). As shown in Figure S32a, b and Figure S33, massive Co<sup>2+</sup> formation in NCM after 150 cycles, which directly reduces the discharge voltage due to the introduction of Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>2+</sup> redox couple which has lower redox potential compared to Co<sup>4+</sup>/Co<sup>3+</sup>. Unlike the NCM sample, the formation of Co<sup>2+</sup> in gradient shell-modified NCM-CS-GMg is significantly inhibited (Figure S32c, d, Figure S34). Moreover, the dissolved TM ions from the cathode material during cycling migrate through the electrolyte and deposit on the Na anode, causing sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410080-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0015_R_d49508345e1438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Environ. Sci.">15</a>, <a href="#anie202410080-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410080-bib-0064_R_d49508345e1438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">64</a></sup></span> The ICP was used to investigate the concentration of Ni, Co and Mn on the Na anode. Figure <a href="#anie202410080-fig-0005">5g</a> displays that the concentration of deposited TM ions of NCM-CS-GMg is much less than NCM, indicating the TM-ion dissolution is controlled in NCM-CS-GMg.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410080-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410080-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have proposed a cathode material with surface gradient desodiation reaction mechanism via constructing the Mg-gradient-distributed shell and Mg-free core. The surface with sufficient Na<sup>+</sup> ions pillars located at Na layers resist TM-ion migration, consequent surface reconstruction and microcracks at high voltage. The residual Na<sup>+</sup> ions also endow NCM-CS-GMg with a stable interface by reducing the formation of Ni<sup>4+</sup> and Co<sup>4+</sup>, alleviating TM-ion reduction and dissolution caused by interfacial side reactions. Moreover, the surface gradient desodiation structure avoids the potential core–shell separation and minimize the capacity loss. The NCM-CS-GMg cathode possesses an ultra-long cycling life of 3000 cycles with a tiny voltage fading (0.127 mV per cycle). This research offers new insight to provide a solution for capacity decay and voltage drop without compromising on the initial specific capacity for oxides cathode.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
            const anchor = li.querySelector('a');
            if (anchor && anchor.textContent.trim() === 'Edit Access Denial') {
                li.style.display = 'none';
